5.4pt;	mso-para-margin-top:0in;	mso-para-margin-right:0in;	mso-para-margin-bottom:10.0pt;	mso-para-margin-left:0in;	line-height:115%;	mso-pagination:widow-orphan;	font-size:11.0pt;	mso-bidi-font-size:14.0pt;	font-family:"Angsana New","serif";	mso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;	mso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;	mso-bidi-language:BN;}</style><![endif]--></p><div class="Section1"><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 86.9pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 3.1pt 0in .0001pt .5in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Code of Criminal Procedure</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 5.75pt 3.1pt .0001pt 6.7pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                              </span><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                   </span>(V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Benefit of doubt</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The prosecution did not prove the guilt of the accused beyond reasonable doubt and, accordingly, he is found not guilty of the offence as charged.<em>Md. Abdur Rashid -Vs.- The State 5 ALR(AD)2015(1) 15<strong>3.</strong></em></span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 115 read with </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> Local Government (Union Porishad) </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Act (LXI of 2009)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.05pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 23(1)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The provision of section 115 of the CPC has conferred jurisdiction in this court to hear revisional application from a suit or proceeding which has been decided by a subordinate court in its capacity as a civil court and in exercise of its jurisdiction under the CPC. The impugned judgment and order, in the instant case, has been passed in an election appeal, by an Election Appellate Tribunal, constituted under section 23(1) of the Local Government (Union Council) Act, 2009. Hence, in this case section 115 of the CPC shall not apply.<em>Khan Zahan AH (Kalu) Patwary-Vs.-Md. Yousuf Khan &amp; others. 4 ALR 2014(2) 248. </em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .5pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 9(3), 31(A) and 290 An Assistant Sessions Judge deemed to be an Additional Sessions Judge-its legal incidences.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">An Assistant Sessions Judge under the proviso to Sub-section (3) of section 9 of the Code of Criminal Procedure has the power to pass higher sentences except the sentence of death in those Sessions cases which were not hither to triable by him but which are now triable by him by deeming and treating him as an Additional Sessions Judge by virtue of the change brought in section 290 and introduction of section 31 (A) of the Code. An Assistant Sessions Judge shall be deemed to an Additional Sessions Judge for this limited purpose only. He has no power to hear appeals, revisions, references and reviews. <em>Tajul Islam and others - Vs.- Mr. Bill al Hossain (Criminal) 2 ALR (2013)(AD) 205.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 35</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 2.15pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Out of awarded sentence the quantum of sentence which has the accused already served out shall be deducted on the application of the provision of section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure. The period in custody before commencement of trial and after commencement of trial be deducted fromthe total sentence under section 35A of the Code of Criminal Procedure.<em>The State -Vs.-Azaharul Islam. 4 ALR 2014(2) 181.</em></span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">      </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 35A - The period of time spent by the accused in custody during pendency of the trial would be deducted from his total sentence.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .15in; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sentence of "imprisonment for life" as used in Bangladesh is utterly a misnomer; indeed. It appears to be an erroneous interpretation. The way it has been interpreted, the word "life" does not bear its normal linguistic meaning. In other words, a person sentenced to imprisonment for life does not necessarily spend his life in prison, although section 45 of the Penal Code defines "Life" as the life of a human being unless the contrary appears from the context. The given interpretation has been arrived at with the aid of section 57 of the Penal Code, which provides that in calculating fraction of terms of punishment, imprisonment for life shall be <em>reckoned as equivalent to rigorous imprisonment for 30(thirty) years. This </em>last mentioned section read with relevant provision of the Jail Code effectively means<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>that<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>a<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>person<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>sentenced to imprisonment for life will be released after spending a maximum of 22 years in prison. Under section 35A of the Code of pro<em>cedure [V of1898] </em>of Criminal Procedure the period of time spent by the accused in custody during pendency of the trial would be deducted from his total sentence. Thus we find that in many serious murder cases where the trial lasts for many years, the accused who is found guilty and sentenced to imprisonment for life gets released after serving a total of 2214 years including the period spent in custody during trial. Hence, the sentence of imprisonment for life imposed at the time of delivery of judgment appears to be a lenient sentence and may in the minds of some appear to be not a proper sentence, especially when some horrific facts are disclosed in evidence.<em>Rokia Begum -Vs.-<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>The State 3 ALR(2014)(1)(AD) 148.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 42</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 42 Public bound to assist Magistrates an the law-enforcing agencies (the Police) reasonably demanding aid while discharging their official duty such as taking or preventing the escape of any person whom such <em>Magistrates or police officer is authorized </em>to arrest<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>an in the<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span><em>prevention or </em>suppression of a breach of the peace, or in the prevention of any injury attempted to be committed to any railway, canal, telegraph or public property.<em>Ms. Ok-Kyung Oh -Vs.- The State 3 ALR(2014)(1) 402.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 61 - The accused person were kept in police custody beyond twenty-four hours in violation of the provision of section 61 of the Code.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 6.0pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Since both the accused Uzzal and Shamim were kept in police custody beyond twenty-four hours in violation of the provisions of Section 61 of the Code, the confessional statements made by them to the P.W. 13 are necessarily involuntary, the High Court Division would like to say that it is on record that the accused Uzzal was arrested at 11 <strong><em><span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">AO </span></em></strong>A.M. and the accused Shamim was arrested at 11:55 A.M. on 01.10.2004 and the accused Uzzal was produced before the Magistrate at 12:35 P.M. and the accused Shamim was produced before the Magistrate at 12:50 P.M. on 02.10.2004 for recording of their confessional statements. So it is seen that there was more or less one hour delay in producing them before the Magistrate on 02.10.2004 for recording of their confessions under Section 164 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. In this context, the High Court Division feels tempted to refer to Section 61 of the Code which provides that no police officer shall detain in custody a person arrested without warrant for a longer period than under all the MR Criminal Forma-04.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 6.0pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span></span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">circumstances of the case is reasonable, and such period shall not, in the absence of a special order of a Magistrate under Section 167, exceed twenty-four hours exclusive of the time necessary for the journey from the place of arrest to the Magistrate's Court. The materials on record do not indicate the time spent for the journey of the accused from the place of arrest to the Magistrate's Court. Of course, it is on record that both the accused Uzzal and Shamim were arrested from the place of occurrence and brought to the Police Station first and thereafter the police interrogated them and in course of interrogation, they made extra-judicial confessions and pursuant thereto, the two knives used in the commission of the offence were recovered from the house of the accused Shamim at the showing of the accused Uzzal. In the facts and circumstances of the case and in view of the discussions made above, it is rather difficult for High Court to come to a conclusive finding about the alleged illegal detention of the accused Uzzal and Shamim in police custody beyond twenty-four hours in violation of Section 61 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.<em>Md. Uzzal Hossain and others - Vs-The State (Moyeenul Islam Chowdhury J) <span style="letter-spacing: 1.0pt;">6</span> <span style="letter-spacing: 1.0pt;">ALR-</span>2015(2)269.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span> Section - 94(l)(b)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Court has power to call for any paper or document in possession of any person, including Banking institutions, for its production before it, which is considered necessary for a proper trial of the case before it. The Court has also the power to summon any person to appear before it and to depose in the case when his evidence is considered necessary for a proper trial and that in the interest of justice. In the instant case the production of the relevant bank document is vitally necessary for proof of the complainant's case and as such the learned Magistrate was not at all justified in refusing to call for the necessary Bank document and refusing to summon the local Sonali Bank Manager as a witness in the case. The learned Magistrate is directed to cause production of the necessary band papers for the purpose of the trial by following the provisions of the Bankers, Books Evidence Act, 1891.<em>Juboraj Goula -Vs.- The State 2 ALR(2013)(HCD) 175.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 145 -Action for preventing a breach of the peace concerning an immovable property.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 1.7pt 2.65pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">In passing an order for attachment of a disputed property and appointment of a receiver, the Magistrate is required under the law to record a finding that exists or existed an apprehension of there serious breach of the peace. In the absence of such a finding, an order of attachment and appointment of a receiver for the disputed property is "contrary to law".</span></p><p class="Style11" style="margin-top: .7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; line-height: normal; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The 2nd proviso to section 145(4) of the Code of Criminal Procedure though empowers the Magistrate to attach the subject -matter of dispute </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 154-F.I.R.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Every information relating to the commission of a cognizable offence given to the Officer-in-charge of a Police Station or any other authorized police officer, and which is first of time, shall be the first information report (F.l.R.) of the case. Information lodged after the commencement of investigation cannot be the F.l.R. but is a statement under section 161 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. <em>Alauddin -Vs- The State 2 ALR (2QB)(HCD) 457.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 161</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Mere delay is not fatal in recording the statements of witnesses. - Mere delay in recording the statements of the prosecution witnesses under section 161 of the Code of Criminal Procedure can not be considered fatal if the evidence adduced by them in court appears to be credible after sifting. <em>Shahjahan Khalifa - Vs.- The State 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 55.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 6.7pt 0in .0001pt 27.6pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">           </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .5pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 161" Last seen"</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The fact of calling and taking away by the accused the three victims one not proved by the prosecution witnesses and they did not tell fact to the I.O. and thus the claim of circumstances is broken and as such Last seen is not proved. <em>Md. Abdur Rashid -Vs.- The State 5 ALR(AD)2015(1) 153.</em></span></p><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 161 read with </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 105</span></strong><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 7.7pt .5pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The occurrence took place on 02-09-2001. P.Ws. 3, 4 were examined by the I.O. on 15-12-2001 and 17-11-2001 respectively, both P.Ws. 6-7 were examined by the I.O. on 30-11-2001 and P.W. 11 was examined after 3/4 months from the occurrence. So inordinate delay in examining the important prosecution witnesses cast a serious doubt as to the truth of the prosecution case and in the circumstances their evidence cannot be relied on and are to be left out of consideration. In view of the settled principle of law the evidence of P.Ws. 3, 4, 6, 7 and 11 should not be relied on and the same are to be left out of consideration.<em>The State -Vs-<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>Pannu &amp; others 1 ALR (HCD)87.</em></span></p><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">       </span></span></strong><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 161 -Statement recorded u/s 161 of the Code is not substantive evidence.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Mere delay in recording the statement of a witness by the investigation officer of the u/s 161 Cr P.C. is mot fatal to the prosecution if the evidence of such witness adduced by them before the Court appears to be credible on sifting of the evidence. <em>Shahjahan Khalifa - Vs. - The State 2 ALR (2013)(AD) 78.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164- The absence of the recording Magistrate as witness has not caused any prejudice to the accused appellant.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 11.05pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The confessional statement of the appellant was recorded by Mazaffar Ahmed, Magistrate 1st Class and was proved in court by Abu Bakkar Siddique, Magistrate 1st Class who deposed as P.W. 7. In his cross-examination he stated that the recording Magistrate was abroad and that he recognized his signature as he is a colleague. It further appears that P.W. 7 was cross-examined on behalf of accused appellant Ayen Uddin, particularly with regard to the recording of the confession and filling up of the form. The Appellate Division do not find any material irregularity in filling the form by the Magistrate. The Appellate Division view all the relevant questions have been put to the accused who made the statement voluntarily implicating himself with the offence. As such the Appellate Division not find that the absence of the recording Magistrate as witness has not caused any prejudice to the accused appellant. Had the Magistrate been called as a witness he would simply have repeated what he has recorded in the form of the confessional statement. <em>Md. Ayen Uddin -Vs.- The State 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 90.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 11.05pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .5pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 24 and 30</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The inculpatory confessional statements of the condemned prisoners Jamir and Piyas as well as of accused Babul Miah (since deceased) corroborated by the attending circumstances and material evidence are sufficient to establish beyond any reasonable doubt that all the appellants Siraj, Rajib, Sohel, Masum, Kabir, Piyas, Jamir and Manik and another Babul Miah (since deceased) pursuant to a conspiracy in furtherance of their common intention of all brutally committed the murder of Akhter Hossain, his wife Sabrina Afroz Julie and their daughter Arna Akhter aged about <strong><em>2Vi </em></strong>years. There is also nothing to show as to why would the confessing accused persons Jamir, Piyas and Babul Miah (since deceased) falsely assigned part of killing to the other co-accused persons Siraj, Rajib, Sohel, Masum and Kabir. <em>The State -Vs.- Md. Sirajul Islam and others 1 ALR (HCD)185.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 5.5pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 164 - Confessional statement.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.95pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Appellate Division observed that the accused was previously taken on remand and did not make any confessional statement and only made the confessional statement after having been taken on remand for the second time, raises doubts about the voluntariness of the confessional statement, especially when the Magistrate who recorded the statement is found to have not made a genuine effort to find out the real character of the confession. The High Court Division noted that the confessional statement was recorded in a very mechanical and casual manner, so Appellate Division does not find any reason to interfere with the decision of the High Court Division. <em>The State -Vs.- Nuru Miah 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 97</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 11.5pt 0in .0001pt 26.9pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">             </span></span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; letter-spacing: -1.0pt;"><br/></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.95pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 173 (3B)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.95pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 173 (3B) of the Code allows for further investigation and submission of a further report with further evidence, oral <strong>or </strong>documentary in respect of an offence, even after a report had been forwarded to the Magistrate under section 173 (1) of the Code. <em>Abu I Bashar Chowkidar - Vs.- Abdul Mannan 6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)61.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 6.5pt 13.45pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; letter-spacing: -1.0pt;"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 2.4pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 173 - Further investigation for Election of further evidence.</span></strong></p><p class="Style4" style="margin-top: 6.5pt; line-height: normal; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The investigating officer left out some eye witness in respect of the offence. Moreso, he failed to record the statements of some important witnesses under section 161 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. The learned Magistrate after considering the consequence of the prosecution case send it for further investigation. At any stage of the proceeding if it appears that further investigation is required for collection of further evidence the case can be sent for further investigation. <em>Pannu @ Md. Pannu Mia -Vs.- The State 5 ALR (HCD)2015(1) 258.</em></span></p><p class="Style4" style="margin-top: 6.5pt; line-height: normal; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 173 and 202 </span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">- A "Charge" corresponds to an "indictment" in English Law and is very much more than a mere form. It is a precise formulation of specific accusation made against a person. Charge has to be framed on the basis of the material placed before the Trial Court and while doing so the Court is not bound by the inquiry report submitted under section 202 of the Code of Criminal Procedure or in a Police challan case by the report submitted under section 173 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. <em>Munna Dey -Vs.- The State 5 ALR (HCD)2015(1) 147</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 4.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 173(3B) read with </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Anti-Corruption Commission Act, 2004</span></strong><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.6pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 32 (1) </span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">- Further Investigating -The Appellate Division held that the High Court Division observed that under section 173 (3 B) of the Code the Investigating Officer does not require any permission from the court concerned for further investigation and hence after completion of further investigation filing of supplementary charge sheet does not suffer from any illegality. The High Court Division has treated the subsequent investigation as a further investigation and the report as a supplementary charge sheet, which is not illegal. <em>Chowdhury Mohidul Haque -Vs.-Anti-Corruption Commission 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 71</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 190(c) - Without cognizance, trial shall be vitiated.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 11.3pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Unless cognizance is taken of the offence against the accused, his subsequent trial, if any, can not be sustainable in law inasmuch as the learned Tribunal Judge did not prima facie hold that there was commission of any offence by him. In such a situation, the trial of the accused shall stand vitiated.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 197</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">No court shall take cognizance of the offence against a public servant under Section 25B(1) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 without the prior sanction of the Government under Section 197 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, AIR 200 SC 2952 paragraphs 17-19): Section 197(1) of the Code does not impose any absolute ban against taking cognizance of the offence, but it only says that the sanction contemplated therein is a condition precedent for taking such cognizance. It obviously is for preventing public servants from being subjected to frivolous prosecutions for discharging their official duties. <em>Md. Abdul Basit-Vs.-The State 1 ALR (AD) 160.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 198 and 561A -- Abuse of the process of the Court - initiation of a proceeding on the complaint of a private party is barred.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 11.05pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is now well settled that taking of cognizance without a complaint in writing, the Court itself in whose Court proceedings the alleged documents were produced or given in evidence is illegal.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 2.15pt; text-align: justify;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 32 (1) </span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">- Further Investigating -The Appellate Division held that the High Court<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>Division<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>observed<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>that under section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>173 (3 B) of the Code the Investigating Officer does not require any <em>permission from the court concerned for </em>further<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>investigation<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>and<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>hence after completion of further investigation filing of supplementary charge sheet does not <em><span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">suffer</span> from </em>any illegality.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>The High Court Division has treated the subsequent investigation as a further investigation and the report as a supplementary charge sheet, which is not illegal. <em>Chowdhury Mohidul Haque -Vs.-Anti-Corruption Commission 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 71.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: -5.85pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 190(c) - Without cognizance, trial shall be vitiated.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt -5.85pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Unless cognizance is taken of the offence against the accused, his subsequent trial, if any, can not be sustainable in law inasmuch as the learned Tribunal Judge did not prima facie hold that there was commission of any offence by him. In such a situation, the trial of the accused shall stand Section 197.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 2.15pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p></div><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section<strong> 32 (1) </strong>- Further Investigating -The Appellate Division held that the High Court<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>Division<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>observed<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>that under section<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>173 (3 B) of the Code the Investigating Officer does not require any <em>permission from the court concerned for </em>further<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>investigation<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>and<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>hence after completion of further investigation filing of supplementary charge sheet does not <em><span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">suffer</span> from </em>any illegality. The High Court Division has treated the subsequent investigation as a further investigation and the report as a supplementary charge sheet, which is not illegal. <em>Chowdhury Mohidul Haque -Vs.-Anti-Corruption Commission 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 71.</em></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.65pt 6.7pt .0001pt .5in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 190(c) - Without cognizance, trial shall be vitiated.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Unless cognizance is taken of the offence against the accused, his subsequent trial, if any, can not be sustainable in law inasmuch as the learned Tribunal Judge did not prima facie hold that there was commission of any offence by him. In such a situation, the trial of the accused shall stand vitiated.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">No court shall take cognizance of the offence against a public servant under Section 25B(1) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 without the prior sanction of the Government under Section 197 of the Code <em><span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">of</span> Criminal Procedure, </em>AIR 200 SC <em>2952 </em>paragraphs 17-19): <em>Section <span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">197(1) </span></em>of the Code does not impose any absolute ban against taking cognizance of the offence, but it only says that the sanction contemplated<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>therein<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>is<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>a condition precedent for taking such cognizance. <em><span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">It </span></em>obviously<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>is<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>for<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>preventing public servants<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>from<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>being subjected to frivolous prosecutions for discharging their official duties.<em>Md. Abdul Basit-Vs.-The State 1 ALR (AD) 160.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 198 and 561A — Abuse of the process of the Court - initiation of a proceeding on the complaint of a private party is barred.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.15pt 17.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is now well settled that taking of cognizance without a complaint in writing, the Court itself in whose Court proceedings the alleged documents were produced or given in evidence is illegal and without jurisdiction. The initiation and continuation of any proceedings at the instant of a private party is nothing but an abuse of process of the Court. <em>Md. Suruj Sikder - Vs.- The State 3 ALR(2014)(1) 341.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 200</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: 7.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Over and above the discretionary provision in section 27 the High Court Division is also of the view that it was the bounden duty of the tribunal below to examine the complainant under section <span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">200 </span>Cr. P.C. There is no provision of naraji petition in Ain VIII of 2000. This provision is borrowed from the Cr. P.C and according to judicial decision naraji is a fresh complaint which means whenever a person files a naraji petiion he ought to be examined under section <span style="letter-spacing: -.5pt;">200 </span>Cr.P.C. Cr. P.c. is applicable to Act VIII of 2000 and by virtue of that application naraji petitions are entertained in Nari-o-Shish cases. There is nothing in Section 200 to require a statement to the effect that the complainant must first approach a police officer. In fact it would be ludicrous to require a complainant to approach the same police before filing naraji, who had previously filed Final Report. So, it was, in the High Court Division is of view, incumbent upon the tribunal below to examine the complainant under oath, without which examination it was not competent to reject the application. By failing to examine the complainant under section 200 Cr. P.C. also the tribunal below misdirected itself and as such High Court Division decided the Tribunal to like compliance after examine the complainant w/o 200 in the Cr.P.C <em>Md. Mokbul Hossain Molla -Vs.-The State 6 ALR - 2015(2)196</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 5.5pt 0in .0001pt 31.9pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 200 and 202</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 8.65pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 200</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> <strong>of the Code provides for examination of the complainant on oath by the Magistrate before taking cognizance of an offence.</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 202 of the Code authoresses the Magistrate to postpone issue of process for the purpose of ascertaining the truth or falsehood of the complaint by way an inquiry or investigation. The legal position that follows from sections 200 and 202of the Code of Criminal Procedure is that a Magistrate or for that matter the Chief Metropolitan Magistrate is not bound to take cognizance of an offence straight away on a petition of complaint filed before him. He can very well send the petition of complaint to the police for investigation and if the allegations are found to be true, he may direct the police to record on FIR against the accused under appropriate section(s) of law. <em>Muhammad Ismail-Vs.-Md. Rafiqul Islam and others 2 ALR (2013)(AD) 218</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"> </p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 200 and 202</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 2.4pt .7pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The impugned Judgment and order of the High Court Division sending the case back to the Chief Metropolitan Magistrate, Chittagong for compliance of the requirements of Section 200, Cr.P.C. thereby suspending the proceeding of the case now pending before the learned Additional Metropolitan Sessions Judge, Chittagong is not sustainable in law as the complainant was earlier examined on oath in the C.R. Case by the Metropolitan Magistrate first and then again by him during the Judicial Enquiry. The Chief Metropolitan Magistrate then received the said judicial enquiry report and being satisfied took cognizance of the offence, issued the process and transmitted the case record to the Metropolitan Sessions Judge for disposal. The Judge concerned was directed to proceed with the case from the stage at which it was stayed by the High Court Division. <em>Muhammad Ismail - Vs- Md. Rafiqul Islam and others 1 ALR (AD) 155.<br/></em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                       </span></span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><br/></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 1.45pt 96.0pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 221 and 222 Framing of Charge.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 6.25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 221 of the Code provides that every charge shall state the offence with which the accused is charged.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 11.05pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 222(1) of the Code provides that the charge shall contain such particulars as to the time and place of the alleged offence and the persons against whom or thing in respect of which it was committed, as may be reasonably sufficient to give the accused notice of the matter with which he is charged. From the charge framing order in the present case it is found that the description of the offence was mentioned, the time and place of the occurrence and the nature of the offence were mentioned, Only specific section of the Penal Code was not mentioned against the name of he petitioner. Since sufficient particulars of the offence were mentioned in the charge to give notice to the accused about the case to be made about at the trial for enabling him to prepare his defence, non mention of the specific section of the Penal Code does not cause any prejudice to the accused petitioner. The grievance of the petitioner on this count cannot be entertained. <em>Dinesh Mondal -Vs.- The State. 4 ALR 2014(2) 22.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 11.05pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> Sections </span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">222(2) 234 read witG</span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>Criminal Law Amendment Act</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">[XL of 1958]</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.6pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 6 (IB) - A person accused of more offences than one punishable under this Act, may be tried at one trial for all such offences.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 7.7pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The provisions of Section 222(2) read with Section 234 (1) require that if there are more than one offences committed over a period of more than 12 months then the offences may not be charged in one charge, whereas Section 6 <span style="letter-spacing: 1.5pt;">(IB) </span>provides that any number of offences punishable under the Criminal Law Amendment Act irrespective of the period over which the offence was committed, may be tried at one trial. Clearly, therefore, the provision in the Criminal Law Amendment Act is not consistent with the provision of the Code of Criminal Procedure thereby ousting the applicability of the provisions of the Code in proceedings before the Court of Special Judge. Hence, all the offences committed over any length of period of time could be tried in one trial upon framing one charge. <em>The State -Vs.- Md. Ibrahim Ali 3 ALR(2014)(1)(AD) 199 </em><strong>ALR.</strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 7.7pt 1.2pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 227 </span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span>Nari-O-Shishu Nirjatan Daman Ain, 2000</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> [</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; font-variant: small-caps; letter-spacing: 1.0pt;">viii</span> <span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">of <span style="font-variant: small-caps;">2000]</span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 7,9(1) and 30 </span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">- The victim declined to be examined medically she admitted in her cross-examination that she met and talked to the inmates of the house including a sister of the accused and her husband as well as other persons who visited the house. When she was taken to another village, she stated that people asked her questions. There is no mention that she complained to them about her abduction or rape. The informant alleged to have narrated the occurrence first of all to one Rokeya Begum, a neighbor who was not examined by the prosecution in the absence of any reason for not calling her as a witness a negative inference can drawn against the prosecution for not examining her. <em>The State -Vs.- Md. Palash 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 84</em></span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                    </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 227</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.9pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">An important aspect of criminal trial cannot lead to acquittal of the accused merely on the ground of technical defect. Section 227 of the Code provides as follows:-</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.9pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Court may alter charge.-</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 18.25pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; mso-list: l0 level1 lfo1; tab-stops: 36.5pt; margin: 8.9pt 0in .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(1)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">   </span></span></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Any Court may alter or add to any charge at any time before judgement is pronounced.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 18.25pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; mso-list: l0 level1 lfo1; tab-stops: 36.5pt; margin: 9.1pt 0in .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif'; mso-fareast-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(2)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">   </span></span></span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Every such alteration or addition shall be read and explained to the accused.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.9pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The State -Vs.- Md. Ibrahim Ali 3 ALR(2014)(1)(AD) 199</span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 228</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Court can proceed with the trial even on the day of amending the charge or adding any new charge if it would not prejudice the accused in his defence. <em>The State -Vs.- Md. Ibrahim Ali 3 ALR(2014)(I)(AD) 199.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 10.55pt 0in .0001pt 30.7pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">             </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 232</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">If ingredients of an offence are disclosed the charge may be altered at any time or a new charge may be framed at any time up to the pronouncement of judgment, giving opportunity to the accused to put in his defence against the new or altered charge. But in no way can he be acquitted if the evidence against him proved his guilt in commission of an offence. <em>The State -Vs.- Md. Ibrahim Ali 3 ALR(2014)(1)(AD) 199.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.2pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section – 241 Discharge of accused.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Facts stated in the application under Section 241A of the Code of Criminal Procedure praying for discharge are nothing but defence pleas and these cannot be considered at the time of framing of charges. In such view of the matter, the learned Sessions Judge committed no illegality in summarily rejecting the . Accused-petitioner’s revisional application u/s 439A of the Code. The High Court Division was also perfectly justified in summarily rejecting the petitioner's application u/s 561A of the Code of Criminal Procedure. <em>Syed Abu Siddique - Vs.- The State 2 ALR (2013)(AD) 62.</em></span><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                  </span><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">    </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.7pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 247</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 8.4pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">For appearance of the complainant on any date fixed for the case initiated on a petition of complaint, the Magistrate shall dismiss the case and acquit the accused, unless for some reason he thinks proper to adjourn the hearing of the of the case to other day. <em>Abu Azam Md. Yunus Miah and another -Vs.- The State and another.4 ALR 2014(2) 60.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.9pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 265C</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 9.6pt .95pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">On perusal of the petition of complaints it appears to the High Court Division that the Bank categorically stated that the accused petitioner issued the cheques in question in favour of the Bank against repayment of loan liability. The High Court Division have carefully perused the applications for discharge of the accused petitioner. It appears that the plea of the petitioner never raised before the trial Court nor it was an issue before it at the time of framing charge. Thus whether the cheques in question were issued in favour of the complainant Bank or not is a disputed question of fact, which can only be thrashed out during trial upon taking evidence. Moreover, The High Court Division does not see any scope of deciding such question in considering the merit of the revisional application. <em>Tipu Sultan - Vs. - The State 6 ALR -2015(2)190</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 9.6pt .95pt .0001pt 0in;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> Section 265C read with </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Nari-O-Shishu Nirjatan Daman Ain, 2000 </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">           </span></span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><br/></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.6pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 28- No requirement in law for recording the reasons for framing charge<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>elaborately</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">-<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>There is no direction either in section 265C or in any other section of the Code of Criminal Procedure that the court/tribunal will have to record the reasons of framing charge also. If the Court/Tribunal, on examination of the records and also after hearing both the sides finds that there are sufficient materials for proceeding against the accused the Court/Tribunal shall frame charge against the accused persons, there is no requirement in law for recording the reasons for framing charge elaborately.<em>Md. Muntasir Mamun Khan -Vs.-The State 5 ALR (AD)2015(1) 77.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.6pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></em></p><p> </p></div></div>
322	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Constitution of Bangladesh, 1972 (Part-1)</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>   6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)143, 6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)95, 1 ALR (HCD) 169, 1 ALR (HCD)169, I ALR (HCD) 221.</p><p><b>Case Year:</b>  1972</p><p><b>Subject:</b>   Constitution of Bangladesh</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  2018-06-24</p><!DOCTYPE html><p><!-- [if gte mso 9]><xml> <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>  <o:RelyOnVML/>  <o:AllowPNG/> </span>Code of Criminal Procedure</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-right: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                                   </span>[V of 1898]</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 403</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 403 of the Cr P.C and Article 35(2) of the Constitution does not apply unless the accused has been tried and convicted or acquitted for the same<em><span style="letter-spacing: -1.0pt;"> 958/</span>Constitution of Bangladesh </em>offence, and two essential conditions there are namely (i) there must have been a trial of the accused of the offence by a competent court and (ii) there must have been a judgment of conviction or order of acquittal. <em>Ms. Ok Kyung Oh-Vs.-Mr. Bo-Sun Park and another<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)143.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 1.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 33.1pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">              </span>Constitution of Bangladesh Article 111</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">To succeed a postulant must show that the Appellate Division resorted to a fundamental error of law, which remains apparent on the face of the judgment. One of the most striking examples would be where the Appellate Division acted per incuriam or overlooked one or more statutory provisions. As the doctrine of stare decisis does not bind the Appellate Division under Article 111 of the Constitution, a review petitioner can not invoke that doctrine. There are authorities for the proposition that fresh evidence, which has bearing on the event under consideration, but despite best efforts, could not be obtained during the original or appellate hearing, can have effect on review hearing.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Muhammad Kamaruzzaman -Vs.-Chief Prosecutor, International Crimes Tribunal 6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)95.</span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 10.55pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Constitution of Bangladesh Article 47A (2)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 156.25pt;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">The Appellate Division is of the view that a review petition filed by those subject to<br/>regimentation clogged by Article 47A (2) of the Constitution, the Appellate<br/>Division is to ensure that in the pretext of review, re-hearing of the whole matter is not initiated.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 156.25pt;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Muhammad Kamaruzzaman -Vs.-Chief Prosecutor, International Crimes Tribunal 6 ALR (AD) 2015 (2)95.</span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; tab-stops: 156.25pt;"><em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span></span></em></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 31.45pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                    </span>Constitution of Bangladesh Article 49</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: .25pt 0in .0001pt 31.7pt;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                    </span>Code of Criminal Procedure</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 1.45pt 1.45pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                                    </span>[V of 1898]</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan; margin: 1.45pt 1.45pt .0001pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: .25pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Section 401 (1)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">It is contended on behalf of the petitioner that the Court may only enquire whether fraud has been committed in procuring the pardon or remission of sentence but in other cases the Court has got no power to interfere or examine or touch the order passed by the President under Article 49 of the Constitution. The Court cannot fold its hand in despair and declare 'judicial hands off. So long as the question arises whether an authority under the Constitution has acted with the limit of its power or exceeded it or the f Bangladesh power has been exercised without application of mind and mechanically or the order in question is a mala fide one or the order has been passed on some extraneous consideration or how far the order is fair and reasonable it can certainly be examined and decided by this Court. Government have got no authority and jurisdiction to exercise the power under Article 49 of the Constitution but it can exercise power under Section 401(1) of the Code of Criminal Procedure to suspend or remit sentences of a convict in suitable cases. Government in exercising power under Article 49 of the Constitution exceeded its jurisdiction. <em>Sarwar Kamal -Vs- The State 1 ALR (HCD) 169.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 9.1pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; text-indent: 33.1pt; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                 </span>Constitution of Bangladesh Article 49</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                            </span>Code of Criminal Procedure</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">                                     </span>[V of 1898]</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 3.35pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Sections 401 (1), 498 and 561A</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">From the order dated 12.04.1993 it is evident that in the said order nothing has been mentioned with regard to the Criminal appeal. In view of the above one feature is very clear that though the petitioner was granted bail on 28.08.1991 in the said appeal till disposal of the same but after disposal of the Criminal Appeal on 03.09.1992 his bail ipso facto stood cancelled and the High Court Division without allowing him any time, directed to surrender to the trial Court. In view of the above, the petitioner ought to have surrender-red to the trial Court withou making any further delay but he did not surrender and as such he became a . fugitive and the order of pardon was made on 12.04.1993 when he was fugitive. The petitioner without surrendering to Court concerned had pursued for remitting his sentence before the President and the Government and ultimately managed to obtain the said order. When is petitioner move the application for under Sections 561A read with 498 section of the Code of Criminal Procedure he was a fugitive from Justice and as such this Court is unable to give any relief to a fugitive to quashing or setting aside the orders dated 30.06.1997 and 31.08.1997 respectively passed by the learned Assistant Sessions Judge, Cox's Bazar. <em>Sarwar Kamal -Vs.- The State 1 ALR (HCD)169.</em></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';"> </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Constitution of Bangladesh Article 102</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 7.45pt; text-align: justify; text-justify: inter-ideograph; mso-pagination: widow-orphan;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman','serif';">Instead of issuance of a Rule in the instant writ petition and, thereby, staying operation of the impugned notice, if we direct respondent No. 2 to allow the petitioners to be accompanied by their lawyers at the proposed hearing and, further, to take appropriate measures in conducting the said hearing in order to avoid any sort of allegations by the petitioners of unfair treatment in complying with the impugned order, i.e. to appear before respondent No. 2 as well as to produce the relevant papers, then justice will be done. Respondents are<em> if Bangladesh </em>directed to be cautious and careful in carrying out the said hearing so that no allegation of harassment by respondents towards the petitioners in conducting the hearing is ever raised. <em>Moshammat Amena Begum and others-Vs.-Anti-Corruption Commission, represented by its Chairman and others I ALR (HCD) 221.</em></span></p></div></div>
